<li><a href="/wiki/Russian_Braille#Additional_alphabets" title="Russian Braille">Uzbek Braille</a></li>
<li>(<a href="/wiki/Uzbek_alphabet" title="Uzbek alphabet">Uzbek alphabets</a>)</li></ul>
</div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="color: black; background-color: yellowgreen;">Official status</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Official language&#160;in</div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409"><div class="plainlist">

For an introductory guide on IPA symbols, see <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA" title="Help:IPA">Help:IPA</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Uzbek</b><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> (pronounced <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="uz-Latn-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Uzbek" title="Help:IPA/Uzbek">&#91;ozˈbektʃæ;<span class="wrap"> </span>ozˈbek<span class="wrap"> </span>tiˈli&#93;</a></span>), formerly known as <b>Turki</b>, is a <a href="/wiki/Karluk_languages" title="Karluk languages">Karluk Turkic language</a> spoken by <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a>. It is the official and national language of <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> and formally succeeded <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_language" title="Chagatai language">Chagatai</a>, an earlier Karluk language also known as "Turki", as the literary language of Uzbekistan in the 1920s.
</p><p>Uzbek is spoken as either a native or second language by 32 million people around the world, making it the second-most widely spoken Turkic language after <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-e26_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e26-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two major variants of the Uzbek language: Northern Uzbek, or simply "Uzbek", spoken in <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkmenistan" title="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Uzbek_language" title="Southern Uzbek language">Southern Uzbek</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Uzbek,_Southern_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uzbek,_Southern-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Both Northern and Southern Uzbek are divided into many dialects. Uzbek and <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_language" title="Uyghur language">Uyghur</a> are sister languages and they constitute the <a href="/wiki/Karluk_languages" title="Karluk languages">Karluk</a> or "Southeastern" branch of Turkic. Uzbek and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_language" title="Azerbaijani language">Azeri</a>, which is an <a href="/wiki/Oghuz_languages" title="Oghuz languages">Oghuz language</a>, are ranked as the most agglutinating (and hence the least inflecting) of all Turkic languages.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>External influences on Uzbek include <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> One of the most noticeable distinctions of Uzbek from other Turkic languages is the rounding of the vowel <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a>/</span> to <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Open_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open back rounded vowel">ɒ</a>/</span>, a phoneme that was shared by the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Tajik_language" title="Tajik language">Tajik</a>, a variety of <a href="/wiki/New_Persian" title="New Persian">New Persian</a>. Unlike other Turkic languages, <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a> is almost completely lost in modern Standard Uzbek, though it is still observed to some degree in its dialects, as well as in Uyghur.
</p><p>Different dialects of Uzbek show varying degrees of influence from other languages such as <a href="/wiki/Kipchak_languages" title="Kipchak languages">Kipchak</a> and Oghuz Turkic (for example, in grammar) as well as Persian (in grammar and phonology), which gives literary Uzbek the impression of being a mixed language.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 2021, the Uzbek government announced that Uzbekistan plans to fully transition the Uzbek language from the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic</a> script to a <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin</a>-based alphabet by 1 January 2023.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Similar deadlines had been extended several times.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> As of 2024, most institutions still use both alphabets.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic languages</a></div>
<p>Uzbek is the western member of the Karluk languages, a subgroup of Turkic; the eastern variant is Uyghur. Karluk is classified as a <a href="/wiki/Dialect_continuum" title="Dialect continuum">dialect continuum</a>. Northern Uzbek was determined to be the most suitable variety to be understood by the most number of speakers of all Turkic languages despite it being heavily <a href="/wiki/Persianization" title="Persianization">Persianized</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> excluding the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Turkic_languages" title="Siberian Turkic languages">Siberian Turkic languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> A high degree of <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutual intelligibility</a> found between certain specific Turkic languages has allowed Uzbek speakers to more easily comprehend various other distantly related languages.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Number_of_speakers">Number of speakers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Number of speakers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Uzbek, being the most widely spoken indigenous language in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, is as well spoken by smaller ethnic groups in Uzbekistan and in neighbouring countries. 
</p><p>The language is spoken by other ethnic groups outside Uzbekistan. The popularity of <a href="/wiki/Mass_media_in_Uzbekistan" title="Mass media in Uzbekistan">Uzbek media</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Uzbekfilm" title="Uzbekfilm">Uzbekfilm</a> and RizanovaUz, has spread among the <a href="/wiki/Post-Soviet_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-Soviet countries">Post-soviet states</a>, particularly in <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a> in recent years. Since Uzbek is the dominant language in the <a href="/wiki/Osh_Region" title="Osh Region">Osh Region of Kyrgyzstan</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> (and mothertongue of the city <a href="/wiki/Osh" title="Osh">Osh</a>), like the rest of Eastern, Southern and South-Eastern Kyrgyzstan (<a href="/wiki/Jalal-Abad_Region" title="Jalal-Abad Region">Jalal-Abad Region</a>), the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_people" title="Kyrgyz people">Kyrgyzes</a> are, too, exposed to Uzbek, and some speak it fluently. This is a common situation in the rest of Central Asian republics, including: the <a href="/wiki/Turkistan_Region" title="Turkistan Region">Turkistan region of Kazakhstan</a>, northern <a href="/wiki/Da%C5%9Foguz_Region" title="Daşoguz Region">Daşoguz Welaýat of Turkmenistan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sughd" class="mw-redirect" title="Sughd">Sughd region</a> and other regions of <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Tajikistan" title="Languages of Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> This puts the number of L2 speakers of Uzbek at a varying 1–5 million speakers.
</p><p>The Uzbek language has a special status in countries that are common destination for <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigration</a> for Uzbekistani citizens. Other than <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Central_Asian_Republics" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Asian Republics">Central Asian Republics</a>, the ethnic <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a> most commonly choose the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a><sup id="cite_ref-themoscowtimes.com_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-themoscowtimes.com-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> in search of work. Most of them however, are seasonal workers, whose numbers vary greatly among residency within the Russian Federation. According to Russian government statistics, 4.5 million workers from Uzbekistan, 2.4 million from <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, and 920,000 from <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a> were working in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> in 2021, with around 5 million being ethnic Uzbeks.<sup id="cite_ref-themoscowtimes.com_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-themoscowtimes.com-21">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Estimates of the number of native speakers of Uzbek vary widely, from 35 up to 40 million. <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnologue" title="Ethnologue">Ethnologue</a></i> estimates put the number of native speakers at 35 million across all the recognized dialects. The Swedish national encyclopedia, <span title="Swedish-language text"><i lang="sv"><a href="/wiki/Nationalencyklopedin" title="Nationalencyklopedin">Nationalencyklopedin</a></i></span>, estimates the number of native speakers to be 38 million,<sup id="cite_ref-Nationalencyklopedin_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nationalencyklopedin-22">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
and the <i><a href="/wiki/CIA_World_Factbook" class="mw-redirect" title="CIA World Factbook">CIA World Factbook</a></i> estimates 30 million. Other sources estimate the number of speakers of Uzbek to be 34 million in Uzbekistan,<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> 4.5 million in Afghanistan,<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> 1,630,000 in Pakistan,<sup id="cite_ref-Uzbek,_Southern_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uzbek,_Southern-8">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> 1,500,000 in Tajikistan,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> about 1 million in Kyrgyzstan,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> 600,000 in Kazakhstan,<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> 600,000 in Turkmenistan,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> and 300,000 in Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>Uzbek language is taught in more than fifty higher education institutions around the world.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_background">Etymology and background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Etymology and background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a></div>
<p>Historically, the language under the name "Uzbek" referred to a totally different language of <a href="/wiki/Kipchak_languages" title="Kipchak languages">Kipchak</a> origin. The language was generally similar to the neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>, more or less identical lexically, phonetically and grammatically. It was dissimilar to the area's indigenous and native language, known as <i>Turki</i>, until it was changed to <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_language" title="Chagatai language">Chagatai</a> by western scholars due to its origins from the <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khanate" title="Chagatai Khanate">Chagatai Khanate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
The ethnonym of the language itself now means "a language spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a>."
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a></div> 
<p>Turkic speakers probably settled the <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Syr Darya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zarafshon" title="Zarafshon">Zarafshon</a> river basins from at least 600–650 CE, gradually ousting or assimilating the speakers of the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_languages" title="Eastern Iranian languages">Eastern Iranian languages</a> who previously inhabited <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Khwarazm" title="Khwarazm">Khwarazm</a>. The first Turkic dynasty in the region was that of the <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">Kara-Khanid Khanate</a> from the 9th–12th centuries,<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> a confederation of <a href="/wiki/Karluks" title="Karluks">Karluks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chigils" title="Chigils">Chigils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yagma" title="Yagma">Yagma</a>, and other tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-sinor_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sinor-33">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Uzbek can be considered the direct descendant of Chagatai, the language of great Turkic Central Asian literary development in the realm of <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khan" title="Chagatai Khan">Chagatai Khan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> (Tamerlane), and the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_dynasty" title="Timurid dynasty">Timurid dynasty</a><sup id="cite_ref-allworth_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-allworth-34">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> (including the early Mughal rulers of the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>).
</p><p>Chagatai was championed by <a href="/wiki/Ali-Shir_Nava%27i" title="Ali-Shir Nava&#39;i">Ali-Shir Nava'i</a> in the 15th and 16th centuries. Nava'i was the greatest representative of Chagatai literature.<sup id="cite_ref-McHenry_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McHenry-35">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Subtelny_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Subtelny-36">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> He significantly contributed to the development of Chagatai and is widely considered to be the founder of Uzbek literature.<sup id="cite_ref-Valitova_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Valitova-37">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prokhorov_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prokhorov-38">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> Chagatai contained large numbers of Persian and Arabic <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>. By the 19th century, it was rarely used for literary composition and disappeared only in the early 20th century.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Shaybani" title="Muhammad Shaybani">Muhammad Shaybani</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1451</span> – 2 December 1510), the first <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Bukhara" title="Khanate of Bukhara">Khan of Bukhara</a>, wrote poetry under the pseudonym "Shibani". A collection of Chagatai poems by Muhammad Shaybani is currently kept in the <a href="/wiki/Topkap%C4%B1_Palace_Museum" class="mw-redirect" title="Topkapı Palace Museum">Topkapı Palace Museum</a> manuscript collection in <a href="/wiki/Istanbul" title="Istanbul">Istanbul</a>. The manuscript of his philosophical and religious work, <i>Bahr al-Khudā</i>, written in 1508, is located in London.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Shaybani's nephew Ubaydullah Khan (1486-1540) skillfully recited the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> and provided it with commentaries in Chagatai. Ubaydulla himself wrote poetry in Chagatai, Classical Persian, and Arabic under the literary pseudonym Ubaydiy.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the Uzbek political elite of the 16th century, Chagatai was their native language. For example, the leader of the semi-nomadic Uzbeks, Sheibani Khan (1451–1510), wrote poems in Chagatai.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The poet Turdiy (17th century) in his poems called for the unification of the divided Uzbek tribes: "Although our people are divided, but these are all Uzbeks of ninety-two tribes. We have different names – we all have the same blood. We are one people, and we should have one law. Floors, sleeves and collars – it's all – one robe, So the Uzbek people are united, may they be in peace."<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Sufi Allayar (1633–1721) was an outstanding theologian and one of the <a href="/wiki/Sufism" title="Sufism">Sufi leaders</a> of the Khanate of Bukhara. He showed his level of knowledge by writing a book called <i>Sebâtü'l-Âcizîn</i>. Sufi Allayar was often read and highly appreciated in Central Asia.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>Uzbek</i> as applied to language has meant different things at different times. 
</p>
<ul><li><i>Uzbek</i> was a <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel-harmonised</a> <a href="/wiki/Kipchak_languages" title="Kipchak languages">Kipchak language</a> spoken by descendants of those who arrived in <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a> who lived mainly around <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a> and <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>.</li>
<li>Chagatai was a Karluk language spoken by the older settled Turkic populations ("<a href="/wiki/Sart" title="Sart">Sarts</a>") of the region in the <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Qashqadaryo_Region" title="Qashqadaryo Region">Qashqadaryo Region</a>, and in some parts of what is now the <a href="/wiki/Samarqand_Region" title="Samarqand Region">Samarqand Region</a>; it contained a heavier admixture of Persian and Arabic and did not have <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a>.</li></ul>
<p>According to the Kazakh scholar <a href="/w/index.php?title=Serali_Lapin&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Serali Lapin (page does not exist)">Serali Lapin</a>, who lived at the end of the 19th – beginning of the 20th century, "there is no special Sart language different from Uzbek.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Russian researchers of the second half of the 19th century, like L. N. Sobolev, believed that "Sart is not a special tribe, as many tried to prove. Sart is indifferently called both Uzbek and Tajik, who live in the city and are engaged in trade.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Khiva" title="Khanate of Khiva">Khanate of Khiva</a>, Sarts spoke a highly Oghuz-influenced variety of Karluk. All three dialects continue to exist within modern spoken Uzbek.
</p><p>After the independence of Uzbekistan, the Uzbek government opted to reform Northern Uzbek by changing its alphabet from Cyrillic to Latin in an attempt to stimulate the growth of Uzbek in a new, independent state. However, the reform never went into full application, and As of 2024<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> both alphabets are widely used, from daily uses to government publications and TV news. Uzbek language hasn't eclipsed Russian in the government sector since Russian is used widely in sciences, politics, and by the upper class of the country. However, the Uzbek internet, including <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_Wikipedia" title="Uzbek Wikipedia">Uzbek Wikipedia</a>, is growing rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_systems">Writing systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Writing systems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Adib-i_sani.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Adib-i_sani.jpg/120px-Adib-i_sani.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="215" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Adib-i_sani.jpg/180px-Adib-i_sani.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Adib-i_sani.jpg/240px-Adib-i_sani.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1760" data-file-height="3160" /></a><figcaption>A 1911 text in the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_alphabet" title="Arabic alphabet">Arabic alphabet</a></figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Fiction_book_in_uzbek2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fiction_book_in_uzbek2.jpg/220px-Fiction_book_in_uzbek2.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fiction_book_in_uzbek2.jpg/330px-Fiction_book_in_uzbek2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Fiction_book_in_uzbek2.jpg/440px-Fiction_book_in_uzbek2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="4000" /></a><figcaption>Covers of translated books in Uzbek. As can be seen, both Latin and Cyrillic scripts are widely used in the country. Most names are also transliterated, for example <i>Robert Luis Stivenson</i> is equivalent to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_alphabet" title="Uzbek alphabet">Uzbek alphabet</a></div>
<p>Uzbek has been written in a variety of scripts throughout history:
</p>
<ul><li>1000–1920s: The traditional Arabic script, first in the Qarakhanid standard and next in the Chagatai standard. This is seen as the golden age of the Uzbek language and literary history.</li>
<li>1920–1928: the Arabic-based <a href="/wiki/Ya%C3%B1a_iml%C3%A2_alphabet" title="Yaña imlâ alphabet">Yaña imlâ alphabet</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Batalden_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batalden-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>1928–1940: the Latin-based <a href="/wiki/Ya%C3%B1alif" title="Yañalif">Yañalif</a> was imposed officially.</li>
<li>1940–1992: the <a href="/wiki/Cyrillic_script" title="Cyrillic script">Cyrillic script</a> was used officially.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Since 1992: Switch back to Latin script, with heavy holdover usage of Cyrillic.</li></ul>
<p>Despite the official status of the Latin script in Uzbekistan, the use of Cyrillic is still widespread, especially in advertisements and signs. In newspapers, scripts may be mixed, with headlines in Latin and articles in Cyrillic.<sup id="cite_ref-Euro_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euro-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> The Arabic script is no longer used in Uzbekistan except symbolically in limited texts<sup id="cite_ref-Euro_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Euro-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> or for the academic studies of <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_language" title="Chagatai language">Chagatai (Old Uzbek)</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Batalden_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Batalden-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2019, an updated version of the Uzbek Latin alphabet was revealed by the Uzbek government, with five letters being updated; it was proposed to represent the sounds "ts", "sh", "ch", "oʻ" and "gʻ" by the letters "c", "ş", "ç", "ó" and "ǵ", respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> This reverses a 1995 reform, and brings the orthography closer to that of <a href="/wiki/Turkish_alphabet" title="Turkish alphabet">Turkish</a> and also of <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_alphabet" title="Turkmen alphabet">Turkmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karakalpak_alphabet" class="mw-redirect" title="Karakalpak alphabet">Karakalpak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_alphabets" title="Kazakh alphabets">Kazakh</a> (2018 version) and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijani_alphabet" title="Azerbaijani alphabet">Azerbaijani</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In 2021, it was proposed to change "sh", "ch", "oʻ" and "gʻ" to "ş", "ç", "ō" and "ḡ".<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> These proposals were not implemented.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2023)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In the western Chinese region of <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, in northern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> where there is an Uzbek minority, the Arabic-based script is still used. In the early 21st century, in Afghanistan, standardization, publication of dictionaries, and an increase in usage (for example in News agencies' website, such as that of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.bbc.com/uzbek/afghanistan">the BBC</a>) has been taking place.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Words are usually <a href="/wiki/Oxytone" title="Oxytone">oxytones</a> (i.e. the last syllable is stressed), but certain endings and suffixal particles are not stressed.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Cleanup" title="Wikipedia:Cleanup"><span title="Wikipedia:Cleanup (April 2022)">which?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Vowels"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Standard Uzbek has six vowel phonemes.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Uzbek language has many dialects: contrary to many Turkic languages, Standard Uzbek (due to being based on one of the so-called Iranized Uzbek dialects<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>) no longer has <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a>, but other dialects (Kipchak Uzbek and Oghuz Uzbek) retain vowel harmony.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<th><a href="/wiki/Open_vowel" title="Open vowel">Open</a>
</th>
<td colspan="2"><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">æ</a></span> ~ <span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ</a></span>
</td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">ɔ</a></span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a>/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close_back_rounded_vowel" title="Close back rounded vowel">u</a>/</span> can have short allophones <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-front unrounded vowel">ɪ</a>&#93;</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_near-back_rounded_vowel" title="Near-close near-back rounded vowel">ʊ</a>&#93;</span>, and central allophones <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Near-close_central_unrounded_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Near-close central unrounded vowel">ɨ̞</a>&#93;</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close_central_rounded_vowel" title="Close central rounded vowel">ʉ</a>&#93;</span>. <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open-mid back rounded vowel">ɔ</a>/</span> can have an open back allophone <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_back_rounded_vowel" title="Open back rounded vowel">ɒ</a>&#93;</span>.</li>
<li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Close_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Close front unrounded vowel">i</a>/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">æ</a>/</span> can become <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Close_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Close central unrounded vowel">ɨ</a>&#93;</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">&#91;<a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a</a>&#93;</span> when the syllable or the vowel is adjacent to the phonemes <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_plosive" title="Voiceless uvular plosive">q</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_uvular_fricative" title="Voiced uvular fricative">ʁ</a>/</span>, and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_fricative" title="Voiceless uvular fricative">χ</a>/</span> (<i>yaxshi</i> "good" <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[jaχˈʃɨ]</span>).</li></ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As a Turkic language, Uzbek is <a href="/wiki/Null-subject_language" title="Null-subject language">null subject</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative</a> and has no <a href="/wiki/Noun_class" title="Noun class">noun classes</a> (gender or otherwise). Although Uzbek has no definite <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">articles</a>, it has indefinite articles <i>bir</i> and <i>bitta</i>. The word order is <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject–object–verb">subject–object–verb</a> (SOV).
</p><p>In Uzbek, there are two main categories of words: nominals (equivalent to nouns, pronouns, adjectives and some adverbs) and verbals (equivalent to verbs and some adverbs).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">Nouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Nouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Plurals are formed by suffix <i>-lar</i>. Nouns take the <i>-ni</i> suffix as a definite article; unsuffixed nouns are understood as indefinite. The dative case ending <i>-ga</i> changes to <i>-ka</i> when the noun ends in <i>-k</i>, or <i>-qa</i> when the noun ends in <i>-q</i> or <i>-gʻ</i> (notice <i>*tog‘qa</i> → <i>toqqa</i>). The possessive suffixes change the final consonants <i>-k</i> and <i>-q</i> to voiced <i>-g</i> and <i>-gʻ</i>, respectively (<i>yurak</i> → <i>yura<b>g</b>im</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> Unlike neighbouring <a href="/wiki/Turkmen_language" title="Turkmen language">Turkmen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a> languages, due to the loss of "pronominal -n" there is no irregularity in forming cases after possessive cases (<i>uyida</i> "in his/her/its house", as opposed to Turkmen <i>öýü<b>n</b>de</i>, though saying <i>uyi<b>n</b>da</i> is also correct but such style is mainly used in literary contexts).<sup id="cite_ref-CEOLOFTW_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CEOLOFTW-63">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Cases

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Verbs"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Uzbek verbs are also inflected for number and person of the subject, and it has more <a href="/wiki/Periphrasis" title="Periphrasis">periphrases</a>. Uzbek uses some of the inflectional (simple) verbal tenses:<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Cognate with <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a> present continuous suffix <span class="nowrap">-(i)yor</span><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">This suffix is likely a borrowing from <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish" title="Ottoman Turkish">Ottoman Turkish</a><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Word_order">Word order</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Word order"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The word order in the Uzbek language is <a href="/wiki/Subject%E2%80%93object%E2%80%93verb" class="mw-redirect" title="Subject–object–verb">subject–object–verb</a> (SOV), like all other Turkic languages. Unlike in English, the object comes before the verb and the verb is the last element of the sentence.
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:3em"><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">Men</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="first person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">1SG</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">kitobni</p><p style="margin: 0px;">book-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="direct object" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">DO</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="singular number" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">SG</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="accusative case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ACC</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">koʻrdim</p><p style="margin: 0px;">see-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="past tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PAST</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="indicative mood" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">IND</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="first person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">1SG</abbr></p></div><p style="display: none;">Men kitobni koʻrdim</p><p style="display: none;">1SG book-DO.SG.ACC see-PAST.IND.1SG</p><p style="clear: left;">I saw the book</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Influences"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The influence of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, and by extension, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, is evident in Uzbek <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">loanwords</a>. There is also a residual influence of <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>, from the time when <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a> were under the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>. There are a large number of Russian loanwords in Uzbek, particularly when related to technical and modern terms, as well everyday and sociopolitical terms. Most importantly, Uzbek vocabulary, phraseology and pronunciation has been heavily influenced by <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a> through its historic roots. It is estimated that Uzbek contains about 60 Mongolian loanwords,<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> scattered among the names of animals, birds, household items, chemical elements and especially military terms.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Dialects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm/220px--WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm.jpg" controls="" preload="none" class="mw-file-element" width="220" height="123" data-durationhint="91" data-mwtitle="WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5b/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm.480p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="480p.vp9.webm" data-width="854" data-height="480" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-width="1080" data-height="606" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5b/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm.m3u8" type="application/vnd.apple.mpegurl" data-transcodekey="m3u8" data-width="0" data-height="0" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5b/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5b/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/5/5b/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm/WIKITONGUES-_Nataev_speaking_Uzbek.webm.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /></video></span><figcaption>A man speaking Uzbek</figcaption></figure>
<p>Uzbek can be roughly divided into three dialect groups. The Karluk dialects, centered on Tashkent, Samarkand, Bukhara, and the Ferghana Valley, are the basis for the standard Uzbek language. This dialect group shows the most influence of Persian vocabulary, particularly in the important Tajik-dominated cities of Bukhara and Samarkand. The Kipchak dialect, spoken from the <a href="/wiki/Surxondaryo_Region" title="Surxondaryo Region">Surxondaryo region</a> through north-central Uzbekistan into <a href="/wiki/Karakalpakstan" title="Karakalpakstan">Karakalpakstan</a>, shows significant influence from the <a href="/wiki/Kipchak_languages" title="Kipchak languages">Kipchak</a> Turkic languages, particularly in the mutation of [j] to [ʑ] as in <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_language" title="Kyrgyz language">Kyrgyz</a>. The Oghuz dialect, spoken mainly in <a href="/wiki/Xorazm_Region" class="mw-redirect" title="Xorazm Region">Khorezm</a> along the Turkmenistan border, is notable for the mutation of word-initial [k] to [g].
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_country">By country</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: By country"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Turkmenistan">Turkmenistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Turkmenistan"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Turkmenistan since the 2000s the government conducted a forced "<a href="/wiki/Turkmenization" title="Turkmenization">Turkmenization</a>" of ethnic Uzbeks living in the country.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> In the Soviet years and in the 1990s, the Uzbek language was used freely in Turkmenistan. There were several hundred schools in the Uzbek language, many newspapers were published in this language. Now there are only a few Uzbek schools in the country, as well as a few newspapers in Uzbek. Despite this, the Uzbek language is still considered to be one of the recognized languages of national minorities in this country. Approximately 300,000–600,000 Uzbeks live in Turkmenistan. Most of the Uzbek speakers live in <a href="/wiki/Da%C5%9Foguz_Region" title="Daşoguz Region">Dashoghuz Velayat</a>, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Lebap_Region" title="Lebap Region">Lebap Velayat</a> and partly in <a href="/wiki/Ashgabat" title="Ashgabat">Ashghabad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Russia"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Uzbek is one of the many recognized languages of national minorities in <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>. More than 400 thousand <a href="/wiki/Uzbeks" title="Uzbeks">Uzbeks</a> are citizens of the Russian Federation and live in the country. Also in Russia there are 2 to 6 million Uzbeks from the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> republics (mainly <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>) who are immigrants and migrants. Large diasporas of Uzbeks live in such large cities of Russia as <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yekaterinburg" title="Yekaterinburg">Yekaterinburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novosibirsk" title="Novosibirsk">Novosibirsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazan" title="Kazan">Kazan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volgograd" title="Volgograd">Volgograd</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samara" title="Samara">Samara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rostov-on-Don" title="Rostov-on-Don">Rostov-on-Don</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perm,_Russia" title="Perm, Russia">Perm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nizhny_Novgorod" title="Nizhny Novgorod">Nizhny Novgorod</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chelyabinsk" title="Chelyabinsk">Chelyabinsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladivostok" title="Vladivostok">Vladivostok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ufa" title="Ufa">Ufa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krasnoyarsk" title="Krasnoyarsk">Krasnoyarsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Omsk" title="Omsk">Omsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Krasnodar" title="Krasnodar">Krasnodar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voronezh" title="Voronezh">Voronezh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saratov" title="Saratov">Saratov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tyumen" title="Tyumen">Tyumen</a>. Signs in Uzbek are often found in these cities. Signs refer mainly to various restaurants and eateries, barbershops, shops selling fruits, vegetables and textile products. There is a small clinic, where signs and labels are in the Uzbek language. There are also illegal signs in Uzbek on the streets of these cities with underground sex services ("<a href="/wiki/Call_girl" title="Call girl">Call girls</a>"). Uzbeks in Russia prefer to use the Cyrillic Uzbek alphabet, but in recent years Uzbek youth in Russia are also actively using the Latin Uzbek alphabet. Small newspapers in Uzbek are published in large cities of Russia.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Some instructions for immigrants and migrants are duplicated, including in Uzbek. Uzbek language is studied by Russian students in the faculties of <a href="/wiki/Turkology" title="Turkology">Turkology</a> throughout Russia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The largest Uzbek language learning centers in Russia are located in the universities of Moscow and Saint Petersburg. There are also many <a href="/wiki/Russians" title="Russians">Russians</a> who are interested in and love the Uzbek language and culture and who study this language for themselves. Uzbek is one of the most studied languages among the many languages of the former USSR in Russia. Native speakers of Uzbek in Russia usually use in their vocabulary a lot of words from <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uzbek_language_researchers">Uzbek language researchers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Uzbek language researchers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Scientific interest in the history of the Uzbek language arose in the 19th century among European and Russian orientalists. <a href="/wiki/%C3%81rmin_V%C3%A1mb%C3%A9ry" title="Ármin Vámbéry">A. Vambery</a>, V. Bartold, Sh. Lapin and others wrote about the history of the Uzbek language. Much attention was paid to the study of the history of the language in the Soviet period. E. Polivanov, <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Baskakov_(linguist)" title="Nikolai Baskakov (linguist)">N.Baskakov</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> A.Kononov,<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> U. Tursunov, A. Mukhtarov, Sh. Rakhmatullaev and others wrote about the history of the Uzbek language among famous linguists.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sample_Text">Sample Text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sample Text"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The following is a sample text in Uzbek Arabic script of Article 1 of the <a href="/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights" title="Universal Declaration of Human Rights">Universal Declaration of Human Rights</a> (with English version in the bottom,) contrasted with a version of the text in Uzbek written in Latin script.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Uzbek Arabic:
</th>
<td dir="rtl"><span title="Uzbek-language text"><span lang="uz" dir="rtl" style="font-size: 125%;">برچه آدملر اېرکین، قدر-قیمت و حقوقلرده تېنگ بۉلیب توغیلدیلر. اولر عقل و وجدان صاحبیدیرلر و بیر-بیرلری ایله برادرلرچه معامله قیلیشلری ضرور.</span></span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Uzbek Latin:
</th>
<td>Barcha odamlar erkin, qadr-qimmat va huquqlarda teng boʻlib tugʻiladilar. Ular aql va vijdon sohibidirlar va bir-birlari ila birodarlarcha muomala qilishlari zarur.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Uzbek Cyrillic:
</th>
<td>Барча одамлар эркин, қадр-қиммат ва ҳуқуқларда тенг бўлиб туғиладилар. Улар ақл ва виждон соҳибидирлар ва бир-бирлари ила биродарларча муомала қилишлари зарур.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>IPA:
</th>
<td>/bart͡ʃa ɒd̪amlar erkɪn, qad̪r-qɨmmat̪ va huquqlard̪a t̪eŋ bɵlɨp t̪uʁɨlad̪ɨlar. ular aql va vɪd͡ʒd̪ɒn sɒhɨbɨdɨrlar va bɨr-bɨrlarɨ ila bɨrɒdarlart͡ʃa muɒmala qɨlɨʃlarɨ zarur/
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>English original:
</th>
<td>"All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood."
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Uzbek_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: References"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734"><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em;">

<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.afghanembassy.com.pl/afg/images/pliki/TheConstitution.pdf">[1]</a> From amongst Pashto, Dari, Uzbeki, Turkmani, Baluchi, Pachaie, Nuristani, Pamiri and other current languages in the country, Pashto and Dari shall be the official languages of the state. <b>In areas where the majority of the people speak in any one of Uzbeki</b>, Turkmani, Pachaie, Nuristani, Baluchi or Pamiri languages, any of the aforementioned language, in addition to Pashto and Dari, <b>shall be the third official language, the usage of which shall be regulated by law</b>.</span>
